  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_d4c6506b-f8eb-423f-8f46-c33151310ba9'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12743113749465619972"><strong>A commanding debut and a poignant coming-of-age story about a devout Jewish high school student whose plunge into the secularized world threatens everything he knows of himself</strong><br/><br/>Ari Eden’s life has always been governed by strict rules. In ultra-Orthodox Brooklyn, his days are dedicated to intense study and religious rituals, and adolescence feels profoundly lonely. So when h</span>
  <span id="freeText12743113749465619972" style="display:none"><strong>A commanding debut and a poignant coming-of-age story about a devout Jewish high school student whose plunge into the secularized world threatens everything he knows of himself</strong><br /><br />Ari Eden’s life has always been governed by strict rules. In ultra-Orthodox Brooklyn, his days are dedicated to intense study and religious rituals, and adolescence feels profoundly lonely. So when his family announces that they are moving to a glitzy Miami suburb, Ari seizes his unexpected chance for reinvention.<br /><br />Enrolling in an opulent Jewish academy, Ari is stunned by his peers’ dizzying wealth, ambition, and shameless pursuit of life’s pleasures. When the academy’s golden boy, Noah, takes Ari under his wing, Ari finds himself entangled in the school’s most exclusive and wayward group. These friends are magnetic and defiant—especially Evan, the brooding genius of the bunch, still living in the shadow of his mother’s death.<br /><br />Influenced by their charismatic rabbi, the group begins testing their religion in unconventional ways. Soon Ari and his friends are pushing moral boundaries and careening toward a perilous future—one in which the traditions of their faith are repurposed to mysterious, tragic ends.<br /><br />Mesmerizing and playful, heartrending and darkly romantic, <em>The Orchard</em> probes the conflicting forces that determine who we become: the heady relationships of youth, the allure of greatness, the doctrines we inherit, and our concealed desires.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12743113749465619972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3238666692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3238666692" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3238666692?book_show_action=true">Aug 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela M" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m">Angela M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4685500-angela-m?shelf=edelweiss-reviews">edelweiss-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3238666692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10283854483806062305">Looking back as an adult on his teenage years Aryeh (Ari) Eden, sets the stage for the novel : “ As a teenager, I felt this neatly encapsulated suffocation of my childhood, the trackless wasteland of tightening circles I inhabited. I felt sometimes as if I existed alone, outside the external world, bearing no true relation to anyone or anything, as if the invisible harnesses that tethered humans to their surroundings had, in my case, come undone.” His life changes drastically from the quiet, sec</span>
  <span id="freeText10283854483806062305" style="display:none">Looking back as an adult on his teenage years Aryeh (Ari) Eden, sets the stage for the novel : “ As a teenager, I felt this neatly encapsulated suffocation of my childhood, the trackless wasteland of tightening circles I inhabited. I felt sometimes as if I existed alone, outside the external world, bearing no true relation to anyone or anything, as if the invisible harnesses that tethered humans to their surroundings had, in my case, come undone.” His life changes drastically from the quiet, secluded, Orthodox Jewish life in Brooklyn when his family moves to Miami, and Ari’s life does indeed come undone. We first see him in Brooklyn where studying the Torah came before any other learning and endeavor, where Aryeh Eden feels alone, spending time at the library, “ This was what I became: a contemplative boy surrounded always by lonely, rigorous books.” Raised in this sheltered community in Brooklyn, not thinking about college, not even knowing what the SAT was, never having had a drink, never having smoked pot, and never having kissed a girl, it was hard to see how Ari will fit in or be accepted by the circle of kids at his new school. Yet, in this circle where drinking and doing drugs are every day things, he is accepted and he goes their way. For a while I kept thinking, he was in with just a bad bunch of kids, but slowly I realized that these bad kids carried burdens of their own and in spite of their behavior, I felt for them. <br /><br />This had a YA feel throughout even though Ari is looking back and telling the story as an adult, but I felt something deeper here, something about morality, about right and wrong, about finding who you are. It becomes more than just a story filled with an abundance of teenage angst over relationships, over grades, over college acceptances. However, I had a hard time getting into the philosophical discussions between Ari and Evan, a brilliant and grieving boy. I had trouble getting into the group discussions led by Rabbi Bloom, head of the school who has singled out these boys for discussions on Jewish thought. It maybe was just too heady for me and because I just couldn’t relate, I feel as if I’ve missed something important. <br /><br />I didn’t miss that there are tragic consequences of this deep thought and deep philosophical questions, on the one hand . On the other hand, I saw the tragic consequences of teenage boys shaped by the burdens they carry, or perhaps the act of a disturbed, grieving, and charismatic young man, pulling his peers into dangerous places . I was very much taken in by Ari and his struggle over who he is, and I always enjoy this kind of introspective narrative. This has been compared to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29044.The_Secret_History" title="The Secret History by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret History</a>, which I haven’t read, but in some ways it reminded me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17333223.The_Goldfinch" title="The Goldfinch by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Goldfinch</a> in tone, in Ari’s search for himself and this story, too, felt profound and sad. In spite of the things that didn’t work for me, I was always interested in Ari’s story and how things would end up for him. <br /><br />I received an advanced copy of this book from Ecco/Harper Collins through Edelweiss.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10283854483806062305" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3244426276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3244426276" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3244426276?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3244426276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6707491018603225251">I really loved this book. A reader doesn’t need to be Jewish to enjoy the intimate nuanced- the conscience-ridden and carefully wrought, candor, humor, or the tragic underlying vulnerability and subtle power....but it helps being Jewish for the cultural flavors.<br/><br/>We meet 17-year-old Aryeh Eden.<br/> “This is what I became: a contemplative boy surrounded always by lonely, rigorous books”. <br/> “Such was the way I received some semblance of an education. I stood out in English classes, if only because an ala</span>
  <span id="freeText6707491018603225251" style="display:none">I really loved this book. A reader doesn’t need to be Jewish to enjoy the intimate nuanced- the conscience-ridden and carefully wrought, candor, humor, or the tragic underlying vulnerability and subtle power....but it helps being Jewish for the cultural flavors.<br /><br />We meet 17-year-old Aryeh Eden.<br /> “This is what I became: a contemplative boy surrounded always by lonely, rigorous books”. <br /> “Such was the way I received some semblance of an education. I stood out in English classes, if only because an alarming number of my classmates flirted with illiteracy. Their parents were content with whatever school managed to teach— astonishingly little— and actually preferred their children study Talmud exclusively”. <br /><br />Leaving Brooklyn didn’t make Aryeh (Ari), Eden sad....<br />“on the contrary, the exhilarating prospect of trading my dreary, uneventful life for something new was, at long last, within reach. what was saddening was the realization that, in time, we stand in emptied houses to learn we’ve never made a mark”. <br /><br />Ari - about to enter his senior year of high school - in a new town very different from where he came from- Zion Hills in Florida....( a secular Jewish Community)...<br />Behavior that would have been unfathomable in Brooklyn, was perfectly normal in Florida. <br />Ari didn’t quite understand how faith functioned in the quasi-secular world.<br /> Noah and Oliver, for instance, despite that they attended a yeshiva high school, had not yet worn yarmulkes in Ari’s presence. <br />Ari wondered which of the families maintained kosher homes or observed Shabbat. He was starting to see the value of adhering to ancient rituals without sacrificing participation in the modern world. ( he would be attending school with ‘girls’ for the first time). <br /><br /> “Ever been clubbing?<br />Ari hadn’t.... but if given enough time, Oliver was sure he could make a degenerate out of Ari. ( the readers will see)....yes? no? <br /><br />Welcome to Ari’s new lifestyle ....<br /> “Sophia and Remi threw themselves into a red Porsche while we loaded into Noah’s Audi. I nearly laughed at the absurdity of the scene: two sports cars, each likely worth more than my house; a cast of wealthy strangers; me, tzitzit thrown over my T-shirt, lagging behind, fleeing from a beach, a real beach”. <br /><br />Ari.....<br /> “I had fallen into a group in which I didn’t belong, lied to my parents, neglected a morning of tefillin, drunk myself into oblivion, shattered shomer negiah and, potentially, been slipped some drugDrunk. After some deliberation, I emerged from Shabbat resolved to separate myself from these strange events, to regain a state of stasis. I had moved from Brooklyn with a desire to start anew, yes, but not like this”. <br /><br /><br />Ari was about to have his first SAT tutor session. His school counselor, Mrs. Sallinger sent him to see Mr. Bearman. <br />Ari extended his right hand. <br />He waved his hand away. <br />“Personal sanitation policy”. <br />NOTE.... a month<br />ago I wouldn’t have barely noticed that ‘sanitation’ sentence.<br />This book was written before the outbreak of the coronavirus....<br />The dialogue continues as....<br />“This is Donna. She’s just finishing, Bearman explained, <br />reaching for hand sanitizer and lathering meticulously. Want some?” <br />“No, thank you”.<br />“No, really, I—“<br />“I must insist, he said, dousing my hands with Purell”. <br /><br />Bearman was 33 years old. He went to Berkeley, dropped out of three different law schools on three different occasions. He took the SAT three times, every year, for the last nine years and had never scored outside the 98th percentile. He demanded cash from his clients and he didn’t report his income. <br />“Sue me”, Bearman says to Ari, on his first visit for tutoring. <br />Bearman took home $130,000 a year, enough to get his mother off his back about burning out of law school and never marrying. <br /><br />Ari didn’t have much of a math background from the religious- (Torah driven- education), in Brooklyn. But... he was smart —And his new school counselor in Florida saw his. ( which was why Mrs Sallinger sent him to see the famous-tutor-Bearman). <br />Bearman gave Ari a practice test to take home.<br />“Next week we’ll go from there” <br />“Capeesh?” <br />“Capeesh, I said stupidly. But is that all we can do today—?”<br />“Yeah, you have to get out now. My next client shoes my ear off and I’d like to grab a burrito before he shows up”. <br /><br />The characters give this novel its heart and soul....<br />We meet Aryed Eden’s new friends... Noah, ( who became Ari’s first true friend he ever had), <br />Rebecca, Oliver, Amir, Evan, Sophia, ( who called Ari, Hamlet- and was Ari’s love interest), Remi, Kayla, Donny,... etc. &gt; <br />...his teachers … Rabbi Feldman, Rabbi Bloom, ....the basketball coach named Rocky, Bearman, his SAT tutor,<br />and parents of the kids: Eddie and Cynthia Harris,( Noah’s parents and Ari’s next door neighbors). <br /><br />During the high holidays, Yom Kippur, while sitting in shul, the Shofar sounded long, defiant<br />blasts. <br />Ari tried thinking about guilt, teshuva, the destruction of the Temple, all the suffering in the world: genocide, poverty, terrorism, human rights violations. Instead, all he could think of was Macbeth, hearing the horns of war a final time, ‘crying’, “My way of life is fall’n into the sere”. <br /><br />One night, after many months of being in their new home, new school, new neighbors new friends, Ari’s father asks him —“if we—are better off in this place?”... in Zion Hills? <br />Ari said yes to his father... yet he tried to contort his voice so that it sounded convincing. <br /><br />All the kids at Ari’s new school had distinct personalities....and their own ‘coming-of-age’ issues. <br />Noah, was the star athlete. Sophia was a gifted musician Evan was the great brilliant thinker. Ari, was “supposed to be the other thinker”, Evan tells him. ( little news to Ari, having been not only an only child, but an organic-loner).<br />School in Florida stretched not only Ari’s secular - math- education but also his social skills. <br /><br /> I really enjoyed the characters in this novel very much.<br />On the surface, or at least for a while, the story had a charming lightness - with mild sadness below the surface .... but then....<br />it got quite emotional: VERY... brought me to tears ... and it became very thought provoking. <br /><br />“Who alone suffers, suffers most i’ th’ mind, <br />Leaving free things and happy shows behind”<br /> — Shakespeare, King Lear<br /><br />Wonderful heartfelt &amp; heartbreaking debut. <br />I love discovering David Hopen. I’ll ‘gladly’ read his next book. <br /><br /><br />Thank you, HarperCollins Publishing. This novel will be available in November. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6707491018603225251" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3570696900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3570696900" />
    <a title="Nilufer Ozmekik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53701594-nilufer-ozmekik"><img alt="Nilufer Ozmekik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559807601p2/53701594.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3570696900?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nilufer Ozmekik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nilufer Ozmekik" href="/user/show/53701594-nilufer-ozmekik">Nilufer Ozmekik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3570696900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4694229092937340711">Well! This was promising, breathtaking, gripping and emotional! Such a riveting, moving, powerful, addictive coming of age story! <br/><br/>I don’t want to compare this book with the other novels! Especially with Secret History! Each stories may have some resemblances but they are both unique perspectives, approaches to the lives of young generations! <br/><br/> I could honestly say: I loved Aryeh Eden ( Ari) from the beginning. It reminded me of my quirky, rebellious, isolated, “I’m not from this universe” teenage</span>
  <span id="freeText4694229092937340711" style="display:none">Well! This was promising, breathtaking, gripping and emotional! Such a riveting, moving, powerful, addictive coming of age story! <br /><br />I don’t want to compare this book with the other novels! Especially with Secret History! Each stories may have some resemblances but they are both unique perspectives, approaches to the lives of young generations! <br /><br /> I could honestly say: I loved Aryeh Eden ( Ari) from the beginning. It reminded me of my quirky, rebellious, isolated, “I’m not from this universe” teenage self! I loved the way he breaks his walls and changes himself by surrounded with a bunch of popular also sad, broken friends. <br /><br /> Ari was living in a secluded, devout life in Jewish Brooklyn community. He never kissed a girl. He never put a drug in his mouth or he never tasted alcohol. He buried his head into his books, living in library, enjoying quietness and peace. <br /><br /> But his father’s promotion force them to move Florida which will change his entire life pattern and his shy, quiet characteristics because he doesn’t just move into a new city, new school environment with rich, cool, popular, mischievous teenagers, he just moved to the entire different planet! He finds his freedom, experiencing new things, posh, fast, luxurious cars, girls, parties, risk taker, reckless friends! <br /><br /> He becomes part of a group with the help of golden boy Noah and he finds himself in a rivalry between he and Evan who is walking at the dangerous path and dragging everyone with him. He’s carrying a compelling burden on his shoulders which slowly diminishes his life energy. <br /><br /> For being part of the group, Ari also gets stuck at this dangerous path, estranging his family. The complex rivalry between he and Evan also gets the attention of Rabbi who runs the school. <br /><br /> I enjoyed the realistic characterization and detailed, well-researched, objective approach to the life in Jewish community. This is genuine self-discovery story of a young man’s questioning the morals, learning the facts of life by getting out of his protective cocoon and walking at the edge of the cliff. It’s about friendship, trust, risks, culture, wisdom. <br /><br /> Rabbi Bloom’s philosophical lessons were intense and a little confusing and complex for me. Some Hebrew phrases, discussions took so much of my mental energy to understand most parts correctly( I have to admit I was mostly lost in thoughts) but overall the storyline about the young man’s evolving life, relations with his peers, adjusting to new environment, dysfunctional family dynamics stole my heart and gripped my attention from the beginning. <br /><br /> My final decision: this is one of my best and illuminating fiction readings of the year. <br /><br />Special thanks to NetGalley and Harper Collins Publishers / Ecco for sharing this unique arc with me in exchange my honest opinions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4694229092937340711" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3388067321" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3388067321" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3388067321?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

          marked it as to-read




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=books-claiming-to-be-like-secret-hi">books-claiming-to-be-like-secret-hi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3388067321" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12824065952788885438">a jewish <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29044.The_Secret_History' title='The Secret History by Donna Tartt' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Secret History</a>? this could redeem 2020 for me.</span>
  <span id="freeText12824065952788885438" style="display:none">a jewish <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29044.The_Secret_History" title="The Secret History by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret History</a>? this could redeem 2020 for me.</span>

<div id="review_3376241030" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3376241030" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3376241030?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=brooklyn">brooklyn</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=florida">florida</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=debut-author">debut-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=debut-novel">debut-novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=religion-judaism">religion-judaism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3376241030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3364312933729399175"><br/>As this coming-of-age story begins, Aryeh Eden is a 17 year-old living in Williamsburg, an Orthodox neighborhood in Brooklyn, with his parents, attending a rundown school and spending his days and nights with either his family or his friends either studying, or observing religious rites and rules. He appears to be neither unhappy nor happy, merely following an uneventful life that was prescribed for him without giving much thought to any alternative. Even with his friends, he seems to be emotion</span>
  <span id="freeText3364312933729399175" style="display:none"><br />As this coming-of-age story begins, Aryeh Eden is a 17 year-old living in Williamsburg, an Orthodox neighborhood in Brooklyn, with his parents, attending a rundown school and spending his days and nights with either his family or his friends either studying, or observing religious rites and rules. He appears to be neither unhappy nor happy, merely following an uneventful life that was prescribed for him without giving much thought to any alternative. Even with his friends, he seems to be emotionally isolated, living apart from the life that exists outside of this neighborhood. He never seems to give much thought to his future, until one day he is forced to consider living somewhere else.<br /><br />When Ari’s father seeks new employment, his parents are concerned about taking him out of the environment where he has lived his life thus far, and moving to a less strict Orthodox neighborhood near Miami called Zion Hills, in Florida. A more worldly neighborhood that bears no resemblance to his Williamsburg neighborhood, a neighborhood that to most appears more glamorous, and is certainly more affluent, although once they move there, they live in a much smaller, less expensive home than their neighbors. Still, it is at least twice the size of their old apartment in Brooklyn, and offers Ari more privacy. <br /><br />He is invited to his neighbor’s pool party almost immediately after moving in, a boy his age – both will be entering their senior year in high school at the same Jewish Academy very soon – a boy who is popular among <i>the</i> popular students, who also happen to be atypically well-read, smart students, but they are also students who enjoy partying perhaps a bit too much. Since they include the basketball players as well as some of the best students, and they know how to contrive and maneuver a story, what trouble they’ve been in up until now has been relatively underplayed. When he arrives at this party, he is both a bit overwhelmed by the absence of yarmulkes, as well as the lack of some protocols he considers standards of living according to how he was raised. At the same time as he feels overwhelmed, perhaps unnerved by these changes, there is also a sense of exhilaration of the freedom he senses, as well. A change in location, for him, brings about new prospects – some of which he fears, but on another level years for. Once school begins, unlike his old school where most of his classmates <i>flirted with illiteracy,</i> he may no longer stand out as one of the smarter students, as he is not only behind in some areas, but among his new classmates he doesn’t stand out quite as starkly in a sea of well-read students who have been raised to strive for elite colleges, and lucrative careers seemingly since birth. What Ari does have that separates him, for me, was a choice to better himself, his future, and his life. It is a choice that he seems to struggle with periodically, with somewhat typical teenage angst, but there is more to him, more to his struggle than that. <br /><br />This has been likened to <i>The Secret History</i> by Donna Tartt, which I haven’t read, so I can’t say. There is an element of this that reminded me a bit of <i>Dead Poets Society,</i> the bond that grows between a group of young boys-on-the-verge-of-manhood, a secretive ‘society’ in its own way. What I can say is that I found this to be a mesmerizing story, the kind where you almost want to look away once in a while, but can’t bring yourself to close your eyes or turn away. <br /><br />Mesmerizing, spellbinding, immersive, beautifully written, thoroughly engaging debut novel that is shared in such an intimate style it makes these people, their experiences and these places come alive. To say that I was impressed seems vastly inadequate. <br /><br /><br />Pub Date: 17 November 2020<br /><br />Many thanks for the ARC provided by HarperCollins Publishers / Ecco <br /><br />#TheOrchard #NetGalley</span>
  <a data-text-id="3364312933729399175" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3318932139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3318932139" />
    <a title="Jessica Woodbury" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4271946-jessica-woodbury"><img alt="Jessica Woodbury" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1415105097p2/4271946.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3318932139?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica Woodbury" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica Woodbury" href="/user/show/4271946-jessica-woodbury">Jessica Woodbury</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4271946-jessica-woodbury?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4271946-jessica-woodbury?shelf=arc-provided-by-publisher">arc-provided-by-publisher</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3318932139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15824973956678967825">It has finally happened. After more than 20 years of books being compared to THE SECRET HISTORY that are, in actuality, not like THE SECRET HISTORY at all, we finally have a winner. In fact, not only is this the most similar book you will ever find, the other books it is most similar to are the works of Chaim Potok. (I suspect I am not the only one who had a passionate devotion to all of these in my teens and twneties.)<br/><br/>Here we have the detached, lonely first person narrator in a new academic env</span>
  <span id="freeText15824973956678967825" style="display:none">It has finally happened. After more than 20 years of books being compared to THE SECRET HISTORY that are, in actuality, not like THE SECRET HISTORY at all, we finally have a winner. In fact, not only is this the most similar book you will ever find, the other books it is most similar to are the works of Chaim Potok. (I suspect I am not the only one who had a passionate devotion to all of these in my teens and twneties.)<br /><br />Here we have the detached, lonely first person narrator in a new academic environment, taken in by a group of charismatic friends who are his greatest sources of joy and torment. Here is the dark, brooding, too-intelligent-for-this-world foil. Here is the always-unattainable, always-vague love interest. Here is study and a search for meaning from ancient texts that turns into a dangerous obsession. It almost sounds too tidy. (There are times when the character parallels are so neat that it's annoying.) <br /><br />But fortunately there are many ways in which THE ORCHARD is drastically different. Our hero, Ari, was raised in an Orthodox Jewish community in Brooklyn. When his father gets a new job he is whisked away to a community of wealthy Jews in Florida, attending a school where the academic standards and the household incomes are vastly higher than what he's always known. Ari is quickly adopted by Noah, an athletic golden boy, and his friends. They are smart and ambitious, they are vastly privileged, and they seem different from Ari in every way. He yearns to belong and as he spends more time with them, he starts to set aside the principles of his faith, distancing him from his family. He finds himself in a sort of competition with Evan, the smartest, the most charming, and the most troubled. Not only is he a rival for the attention of the Rabbi who runs the school, but for the romantic affection of Sophia Winter, the of course beautiful and of course sad piano prodigy.<br /><br />The good news is that what you love in THE SECRET HISTORY you will love here. The bad news is, that if you've found you've aged out of THE SECRET HISTORY, this will treat you the same way. I tried to reread it a year ago and had to stop about halfway through. The book was the same, but I was different and I couldn't summon the same depth of feeling I'd once had. Here while I enjoyed THE ORCHARD very much, I found myself pestered by the same kinds of problems. Sophia, in particular, so empty and so perfect, had me frustrated that she is a type and not a person. That said, THE ORCHARD is much better at setting up its third act, and it has a significantly more satisfying ending. All that off-page business in TSH always frustrated me. <br /><br />Reading this on an e-reader is ideal. Hopen throws us into the deep end of Jewish culture, belief, and philosophy. Especially in the first half, I was regularly clicking on a word (the definition wasn't useful, but the Wikipedia entry my Kindle pulled up almost always answered my question). Some people will find this frustrating. I don't, I would've found the constant explaining tiresome. The depths Hopen takes us to in his thought is also much appreciated and an improvement on TSH. For a similar book to work, you need a particularly robust world of thought and morality to enter, and what better than the centuries of Jewish scholarship we get to explore here? Despite my frustrations with the book, the setting and culture is a real strength, even if the characters can feel a bit flat.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15824973956678967825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3359468924" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3359468924" />
    <a title="Dahlia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4688700-dahlia"><img alt="Dahlia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442886197p2/4688700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3359468924?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dahlia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dahlia" href="/user/show/4688700-dahlia">Dahlia</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=2020-releases">2020-releases</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4688700-dahlia?shelf=jewish">jewish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3359468924" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17555149791981292365">So! Many! Thoughts! But for me the most important one was how cool it was to actually see a book that reflected my own upbringing, that spoke in my language(s) and engaged with my world. For that reason alone I am pushing it on every Modern Orthodox reader I know, even though the characters of course also remind me of the most annoying guys from said upbringing. But still! Aizeh chavaya. <br/><br/>Separately, I did enjoy the book and do feel it delivered to an extent on the promise of its premise. I loved</span>
  <span id="freeText17555149791981292365" style="display:none">So! Many! Thoughts! But for me the most important one was how cool it was to actually see a book that reflected my own upbringing, that spoke in my language(s) and engaged with my world. For that reason alone I am pushing it on every Modern Orthodox reader I know, even though the characters of course also remind me of the most annoying guys from said upbringing. But still! Aizeh chavaya. <br /><br />Separately, I did enjoy the book and do feel it delivered to an extent on the promise of its premise. I loved watching Ari's personal realizations about his feelings on his past as he strayed further off the derech, and I liked the glimpses at his family dynamics and seeing everyone drift onto different pages. I'm less interested in philosophical stuff but knew it would be present and appreciated that this didn't turn into one of those books where suddenly everyone is fluent in Schopenhauer. (God, just typing "Schopenhauer is bringing me back to one of Those Boys from school.) And I am alllllways here for biblical and Talmudic references, so that was fun. <br /><br />But on to the climax and my related feelings, which I think is obvious the second you open the book but I'll put in spoiler tags anyway, for anyone who wants to go in knowing absolutely nothing. <br /> <br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ So, I didn't get that "The Orchard" referred to the story of Pardes (it's not a direct translation, though it may be the most common one for this particular instance) until I saw the epigram, and once you know that you're just kind of waiting for the inevitable analog. (Like I said, that's really not a spoiler because the epigram is RIGHT THERE and gave the book its title. And frankly, I think that's an awesome idea, especially for a TSH comp.) That isn't a bad thing, but for me the best execution of the stories where you know The Scene is coming is the kind that makes you feel like you're getting slowly closed in, a little anxious, a little manipulated, a lot of foreshadowing, and this was actually a lot more coming of age stuff than I expected - essentially, much more about Ari's personal character arc than the effect of a group dynamic with a charismatic leader, which is something that despite the TSH comp is never really developed to full effect. And this isn't me trying to turn the book into something other than its goal - every element from the TSH comp playbook is there, including a group especially for these boys. Basically, there's just never any pretense that more than two of the boys ever matter, which I think does a great disservice to everything revolving around the "orchard" concept from lead-up right through the ending. <br /><br />Also, with regard to Evan...while I know "show, don't tell" isn't always apt advice for writing, in this case his impressiveness is entirely reliant on the reader (usually through characters telling Ari) being told how amazing he is. I just don't think he was portrayed nearly charismatic enough for the role he plays in the story, and even though there are good reasons he doesn't embody that <em>now</em>, I don't think his character works well without us having some understanding of what made him such a giant in the past. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I'm also still scratching my head about the romance arcs - I'm not sure what they were going for (representing different sides of Ari? Just more conflict between Ari and Evan? not in love with either of those possibilities), so I look forward to reading more thoughts on that from others. <br /><br />Anyway, this is a book I'm dying to discuss with more people, especially coming from the relevant background, so I do hope more read it and I can't wait to see more thoughts! (And also because I have way more thoughts on the ending but they're spoilerier than I want to put here.) </span>
  <a data-text-id="17555149791981292365" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3642263227" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6462385630556456052">A coming of age story of a young Jewish man who moves from Brooklyn to a community in Florida. His new group of posh friends depict different levels of observance and faith. A complex novel with philosophy, Judaism and spirituality as part of the boy's high school experience. There is a love triangle full of angst and some predictable storylines but otherwise a very good read.<br/><br/>Copy provided by the publisher and NetGalley</span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3642263227">

<div id="review_3408106141" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3408106141" />
    <a title="Anneke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6569017-anneke"><img alt="Anneke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589938347p2/6569017.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3408106141?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anneke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anneke" href="/user/show/6569017-anneke">Anneke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6569017-anneke?shelf=netgalley-read">netgalley-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3408106141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2567525073780193897">Book Review: The Orchard<br/>Author: David Hopen<br/>Publisher: HarperCollins/Eco<br/>Publication Date: November 17, 2020<br/>Review Date: June 23, 2020<br/><br/>I received a free copy of this book from NetGalley in exchange for an honest review. <br/><br/>From the blurb:<br/>“A commanding debut and a poignant coming-of-age story about a devout Jewish high school student whose plunge into the secularized world threatens everything he knows of himself<br/><br/>Ari Eden’s life has always been governed by strict rules. In ultra-Orthodox Brooklyn, his d</span>
  <span id="freeText2567525073780193897" style="display:none">Book Review: The Orchard<br />Author: David Hopen<br />Publisher: HarperCollins/Eco<br />Publication Date: November 17, 2020<br />Review Date: June 23, 2020<br /><br />I received a free copy of this book from NetGalley in exchange for an honest review. <br /><br />From the blurb:<br />“A commanding debut and a poignant coming-of-age story about a devout Jewish high school student whose plunge into the secularized world threatens everything he knows of himself<br /><br />Ari Eden’s life has always been governed by strict rules. In ultra-Orthodox Brooklyn, his days are dedicated to intense study and religious rituals, and adolescence feels profoundly lonely. So when his family announces that they are moving to a glitzy Miami suburb, Ari seizes his unexpected chance for reinvention.<br /><br />Enrolling in an opulent Jewish academy, Ari is stunned by his peers’ dizzying wealth, ambition, and shameless pursuit of life’s pleasures. When the academy’s golden boy, Noah, takes Ari under his wing, Ari finds himself entangled in the school’s most exclusive and wayward group. These friends are magnetic and defiant—especially Evan, the brooding genius of the bunch, still living in the shadow of his mother’s death.<br /><br />Influenced by their charismatic rabbi, the group begins testing their religion in unconventional ways. Soon Ari and his friends are pushing moral boundaries and careening toward a perilous future—one in which the traditions of their faith are repurposed to mysterious, tragic ends.<br /><br />Mesmerizing and playful, heartrending and darkly romantic, The Orchard probes the conflicting forces that determine who we become: the heady relationships of youth, the allure of greatness, the doctrines we inherit, and our concealed desires.”<br /><br />I know it’s a ways until publication of this book, but it looked so interesting that I didn’t want to wait to read it. <br /><br />This is a complex and deep novel. A complex plot with beautiful character portrayals. The setting is captivating and for the most part, the book was a very satisfying read. <br /><br />However, for me, the book veered off the rails in the last quarter of the story. The author introduced an unexpected, ongoing philosophical discussion between the 5 main characters and their high school principal. And he also turned abruptly the book into a tragedy in the final quarter. <br /><br />The veer into the deep philosophical discussion and the tragic plot ending was disorienting. What had started as an enjoyable book suddenly took an unexpected turn that I found unnecessary and unfortunate. <br /><br />So, what I initially thought was a 5-Star book ended with 3 stars and I’m not sure if I would recommend reading it. <br /><br />Thank you to HarperCollins/Eco for access to this interesting book. Best of luck to the author. <br /><br />This review will be posted on NetGalley, Goodreads and Amazon. <br /><br />#netgalley #harpercollinseco #theorchard #davidhopen</span>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3441189033" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/170420-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1228387079p2/170420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3441189033?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/170420-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/170420-laura?shelf=2020-mt-bookpile">2020-mt-bookpile</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3441189033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17849857187441183717">This had so much promise, but the constant digressions into discussions of Gemara and philosophy stop the action and dragged events out for far too long. It also may be difficult to follow some of the plot if you aren't familiar with Judaism (and specifically, the frum life of Modern Orthodox Jews). This isn't <i>The Chosen</i>, although there are some reviews comparing <i>The Orchard</i> to it; yes, we have a fish out of water and deep discussions, but Ari/Aryeh/Andrew/Drew's life in Zion Hills is more like </span>
  <span id="freeText17849857187441183717" style="display:none">This had so much promise, but the constant digressions into discussions of Gemara and philosophy stop the action and dragged events out for far too long. It also may be difficult to follow some of the plot if you aren't familiar with Judaism (and specifically, the frum life of Modern Orthodox Jews). This isn't <i>The Chosen</i>, although there are some reviews comparing <i>The Orchard</i> to it; yes, we have a fish out of water and deep discussions, but Ari/Aryeh/Andrew/Drew's life in Zion Hills is more like Danny spending a lot of time with Reuven, but in <i>The Chosen</i> we see more of the reverse (Modern Orthodox to Hasidic) than here.<br /><br />Once you get past those discussions and the religious aspects, this is a coming of age story about a group of boys trying to survive senior year of high school, get into college, date, etc.. They're all stereotypes, with little growth or change during the course of the book. Some of the adults are complicit, some are simply absent, none are responsible. It also took me by surprise that in this area of Florida, basketball was an academic year-long sport. While it served a plot point, it definitely didn't ring true.<br /><br />eARC provided by publisher.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3610045153" />
    <a title="BookBlanketFort" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4593665-bookblanketfort"><img alt="BookBlanketFort" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3610045153?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BookBlanketFort" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BookBlanketFort" href="/user/show/4593665-bookblanketfort">BookBlanketFort</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3610045153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3417267672977057980">I have mixed feelings on this book. The Orchard is the story of a teenaged boy finding his faith when he moves from an Orthodox Jewish community in New York to a wealthy, less religious Jewish community in Florida. The book takes us through Ari’s senior year of high school, as he drifts away from his strict Jewish practices toward drinking, drugs, and hanging out with a new group of friends. Meanwhile, the students prepare for college and learn about the Jewish faith at their Jewish prep school.</span>
  <span id="freeText3417267672977057980" style="display:none">I have mixed feelings on this book. The Orchard is the story of a teenaged boy finding his faith when he moves from an Orthodox Jewish community in New York to a wealthy, less religious Jewish community in Florida. The book takes us through Ari’s senior year of high school, as he drifts away from his strict Jewish practices toward drinking, drugs, and hanging out with a new group of friends. Meanwhile, the students prepare for college and learn about the Jewish faith at their Jewish prep school. The writer capably examines high school friendships and popularity. It was painful and fascinating to observe social hierarchy, dating, and college application process through the students’ eyes.<br /><br />There were several things I did not like. For me, this book was too long. There was also too much dense discussion of the philosophical underpinnings of the Jewish faith and various Judaic religious texts. For the right reader, these would be very interesting, but it was too dense for me for a novel.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3263922284" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3263922284" />
    <a title="Melinda Worfolk" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10936-melinda-worfolk"><img alt="Melinda Worfolk" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502689733p2/10936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3263922284?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melinda Worfolk" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melinda Worfolk" href="/user/show/10936-melinda-worfolk">Melinda Worfolk</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10936-melinda-worfolk?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10936-melinda-worfolk?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10936-melinda-worfolk?shelf=advance-copy">advance-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3263922284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7978558710549428240">There are inevitable comparisons to be made between Donna Tartt's <em>The Secret History</em> and David Hopen's book <em>The Orchard</em>: both are narrated in the first person by an older character looking back at his younger self; both are set in elite educational institutions with a smaller exclusive group of friends as the focus. Both feature characters interested in discussing The Big Questions: what is the purpose of life? What does it mean to be a good person? What is the role of spirituality in everyday l</span>
  <span id="freeText7978558710549428240" style="display:none">There are inevitable comparisons to be made between Donna Tartt's <em>The Secret History</em> and David Hopen's book <em>The Orchard</em>: both are narrated in the first person by an older character looking back at his younger self; both are set in elite educational institutions with a smaller exclusive group of friends as the focus. Both feature characters interested in discussing The Big Questions: what is the purpose of life? What does it mean to be a good person? What is the role of spirituality in everyday life? What does it take to drive someone to madness and destruction?<br /><br />According to the publisher's description, the title is a reference to "a Talmudic legend in which four rabbis enter 'an orchard' and emerge fundamentally changed." Judaism plays a significant role in this novel; the narrator, Ari, is an Orthodox Jewish teenager whose parents suddenly uproot themselves from Williamsburg, New York City, and move to Florida, where Ari is enrolled in a competitive, academically oriented Jewish high school. There, despite (because of?) his clear outsider status, he is quickly included in the school's "golden group" of young men: athletic, good looking, academically gifted. Among the group is Evan, intense, bitter, and prone to lashing out at those who express concern for him. Ari never stops feeling uneasy around Evan, despite observing that the others in the group clearly care about him. <br /><br />It's best not to know too much about the plot before going in, but since the publishers (and I) have already compared it to <em>The Secret History</em>, you can probably guess that there will be conflict, secrets, and life-altering events.<br /><br />What I enjoyed about the book: 1. The dialogue (and there's a lot of it) is well written. 2. The details about Jewish practices at home and at the school were really interesting to me as an outsider. 3. I also liked the description of Ari's initial adjustment period when moving from ultra-Orthodox Williamsburg life to the shockingly permissive life in the Modern Orthodox community in Florida. 4. The last section of the novel, where the real action begins (and ends), kept me turning the page.<br /><br />What left me dissatisfied: 1. Ari is hard to connect with as a protagonist. His voice is strangely distant and cool (perhaps because he is supposed to be narrating it from adulthood?), and it just didn't work for me. 2. The middle 50% of the book is quite slow. A lot happens at the beginning and end of the book, and those were the parts I was most engaged by, but I found myself wishing in the middle that it would pick up and that something would happen. 3. I would have liked to have seen more depth to the female characters, particularly Ari's mother and Sophia. Unfortunately, Sophia comes across mostly as an Alluring Mysterious Beautiful Girl of Sadness.<br /><br />After finishing <i>The Orchard</i>, I am curious whether I would enjoy <i>The Secret History</i> as much now as I did when I read it nearly 20 years ago. I loved it at the time, but something tells me I might not be able to connect with it as much now. And perhaps that is the issue I had with <i>The Orchard</i>.<br /><br />Thank you to the publisher, Ecco, for providing the advance review copy via Edelweiss+ in exchange for an honest review. A copy of this review is also on the Edelweiss+ site and my blog.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7978558710549428240" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3416793983" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3416793983" />
    <a title="Libby Horwitz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6970476-libby-horwitz"><img alt="Libby Horwitz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547411553p2/6970476.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3416793983?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Libby Horwitz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Libby Horwitz" href="/user/show/6970476-libby-horwitz">Libby Horwitz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6970476-libby-horwitz?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6970476-libby-horwitz?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3416793983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12416442641166887551">Thank you Harper Collins and Netgalley for the copy in response for an honest review.<br/><br/>Equal parts literary fiction, bildungsroman, thriller, and philosophical religious questioning, The Orchard is based on a Jewish legend of four rabbis who enter “Pardes.” The Orchard follows Aryeh Eden as he moves from the frum Orthodox Jewish Brooklyn community to the less strict Modern Orthodox community in Miami (although the community felt much more Reform or Conservative to me and not Modern Orthodox). Ther</span>
  <span id="freeText12416442641166887551" style="display:none">Thank you Harper Collins and Netgalley for the copy in response for an honest review.<br /><br />Equal parts literary fiction, bildungsroman, thriller, and philosophical religious questioning, The Orchard is based on a Jewish legend of four rabbis who enter “Pardes.” The Orchard follows Aryeh Eden as he moves from the frum Orthodox Jewish Brooklyn community to the less strict Modern Orthodox community in Miami (although the community felt much more Reform or Conservative to me and not Modern Orthodox). There Aryeh befriends a group of boys, falls for Sophia, and has religious tutoring from Rabbi Bloom. All of these plots are intertwined and catalysts for different events. <br /><br />I have very mixed feelings on the Orchard, but ultimately it won me over. I was immediately grabbed by the book, and Hopen is excellent at both setting a stage and building and maintaining tension. The book is incredibly atmospheric. The eventual release of tension was satisfying and unexpected.I liked the unique choice and interpretation of the source material. Evan was the most interesting character, and his grappling with religious questions were the most successful part of the book for me. There are actual interesting religious questions asked in the book, and I loved seeing this integral aspect of Judaism represented in fiction. <br /><br />I did however have some major issues with the book, which led me to almost stop reading it. Hopen has an intense attention to detail, when employed well it clearly sets the scene, however, it often feels misplaced, distracting, and unbelievable. The dialogue, especially with teachers, was cliche. The characters, especially the women, felt underdeveloped; while reading them I often pictured them with black holes instead of eyes. The book felt too long, there were plot points that could have been entirely omitted (looking at you basketball scenes), and from 20% through 40% the book was repetitive and redundant (party, mope, repeat). The second half of the book ended up redeeming it for me, as the plot and religious philosophical questioning picked up and led to a very satisfying book ending. <br /><br />I would definitely recommend this book for fans of moody teenage television shows such as Riverdale, or those who enjoy religious philosophical questions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12416442641166887551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3556754024" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3556754024" />
    <a title="Bargain Sleuth Book Reviews" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3017535-bargain-sleuth-book-reviews"><img alt="Bargain Sleuth Book Reviews" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594325697p2/3017535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3556754024?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bargain Sleuth Book Reviews" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bargain Sleuth Book Reviews" href="/user/show/3017535-bargain-sleuth-book-reviews">Bargain Sleuth Book Reviews</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3017535-bargain-sleuth-book-reviews?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3017535-bargain-sleuth-book-reviews?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3556754024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2490859083600175922">For this and other book reviews, visit <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.bargain-sleuth.com'>www.bargain-sleuth.com</a><br/><br/>I’m not sure what drew me to The Orchard when NetGalley offered it to me. Perhaps it was the coming-of-age story, perhaps it was the fish-out-of-water story, perhaps it was because I wanted to read about a religion other than my own. I received an Advanced Reader’s Copy in exchange for my honest opinion. The Orchard will be released to the general public on November 17, 2020.<br/><br/>From the publisher:<br/><br/>“A commanding debut and a poignant coming-</span>
  <span id="freeText2490859083600175922" style="display:none">For this and other book reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bargain-sleuth.com">www.bargain-sleuth.com</a><br /><br />I’m not sure what drew me to The Orchard when NetGalley offered it to me. Perhaps it was the coming-of-age story, perhaps it was the fish-out-of-water story, perhaps it was because I wanted to read about a religion other than my own. I received an Advanced Reader’s Copy in exchange for my honest opinion. The Orchard will be released to the general public on November 17, 2020.<br /><br />From the publisher:<br /><br />“A commanding debut and a poignant coming-of-age story about a devout Jewish high school student whose plunge into the secularized world threatens everything he knows of himself<br /><br />Ari Eden’s life has always been governed by strict rules. In ultra-Orthodox Brooklyn, his days are dedicated to intense study and religious rituals, and adolescence feels profoundly lonely. So when his family announces that they are moving to a glitzy Miami suburb, Ari seizes his unexpected chance for reinvention.<br /><br />Enrolling in an opulent Jewish academy, Ari is stunned by his peers’ dizzying wealth, ambition, and shameless pursuit of life’s pleasures. When the academy’s golden boy, Noah, takes Ari under his wing, Ari finds himself entangled in the school’s most exclusive and wayward group. These friends are magnetic and defiant—especially Evan, the brooding genius of the bunch, still living in the shadow of his mother’s death.<br /><br />Influenced by their charismatic rabbi, the group begins testing their religion in unconventional ways. Soon Ari and his friends are pushing moral boundaries and careening toward a perilous future—one in which the traditions of their faith are repurposed to mysterious, tragic ends.”<br /><br />I had a really hard time getting into The Orchard. I’m not Jewish, and there is so much terminology with which I am unfamiliar, I had to make educated guesses on what certain words meant, or spend extra time looking them up. I disagree with other reviewers who say you don’t have to be Jewish to love this story. I think this story was written for a particular audience, and as a Roman Catholic, middle-aged woman, I’m not it. And I don’t know if it was because of that, but I didn’t realize until I was 10% into the book, when someone mentions texting, that the book takes places in the modern day; I honestly thought it was written about the 1950s or so, based on Ari’s strict Orthodox upbringing.<br /><br />Maybe I wasn’t in the mood for teen angst, but I never really found myself immersed in the book. I thought it implausible that Ari would fall so easily into the gang of “cool people” just because he lived next door to Noah. In my high school world, especially senior year, it would be very hard to break into that exclusive circle of friends who had been together for years. And the interest from Sophie, the smartest, most popular, prettiest girl (and class president) seemed disingenuous.<br /><br />And I don’t think any of my distance to the book had anything to do with the Jewish faith discussed in the book. As an adult, I re-read Catcher in the Rye and The Outsiders and found the kids to be a bunch of whiny kids, so clearly, I am not the target audience (although when I was a teen, I loved those same books).<br /><br />About half way through the book, Hopen throws us into the deep end of Jewish culture, belief, and philosophy, and I just couldn’t get into it. Philosophizing is not for me. Like I said, maybe I wasn’t in a teen angst mood when I read it, but as an adult, I couldn’t connect with Ari or any of his friends, which is surprising. Evan’s mother died and he’s sort of in a tailspin, and the same thing happened to me in high school (my dad died freshman year), but I couldn’t feel sorry for his self-destructive path. I got angry at him for acting out, so I guess that’s a sign of good writing if it extracts such strong feelings from the reader.<br /><br />I tried, I really did, but I just didn’t enjoy this book. I’ll keep expanding my outlook and reading about other cultures and relgions, but The Orchard just didn’t do it for me. If you are of the Jewish faith and are looking for a YA book that discusses being an Orthodox Jew versus a more modern interpretation of the faith, this might be the book for you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2490859083600175922" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3400526674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3400526674" />
    <a title="Susan Ballard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28270573-susan-ballard"><img alt="Susan Ballard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526769314p2/28270573.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3400526674?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan Ballard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan Ballard" href="/user/show/28270573-susan-ballard">Susan Ballard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28270573-susan-ballard?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3400526674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4152163270360638900">4.5 / 5 🌟<br/><br/>This is a dense and pensive coming-of-age novel. Although Orthodox Judaism is at the foundation of this story, the well-educated, wealthy, and popular teenagers who test their boundaries are the true driving force. <br/><br/>Ari Eden has been raised in a very strict Orthodox family. When his father decides to move them from Brooklyn to an opulent suburb of Miami, Ari sees this as his chance to start fresh, to actually make something of himself. <br/><br/>To Ari’s surprise, the Jewish Academy’s star athlete</span>
  <span id="freeText4152163270360638900" style="display:none">4.5 / 5 🌟<br /><br />This is a dense and pensive coming-of-age novel. Although Orthodox Judaism is at the foundation of this story, the well-educated, wealthy, and popular teenagers who test their boundaries are the true driving force. <br /><br />Ari Eden has been raised in a very strict Orthodox family. When his father decides to move them from Brooklyn to an opulent suburb of Miami, Ari sees this as his chance to start fresh, to actually make something of himself. <br /><br />To Ari’s surprise, the Jewish Academy’s star athlete, Noah, befriends him and welcomes Ari into the inner circle. Ari finds this group to be charismatic and ambitious, but also defiant. But Ari is not accustomed to having a circle of friends and being included, so soon following the rituals and traditions are set aside for earthly pleasures.<br /><br />This group of young men are true intellects, especially Evan who has suffered a great loss recently. The head Rabbi of the academy pulls the group together for weekly group sessions. Here they begin to delve into the deep and mysterious questions and mores of religion. But they begin to twist ideologies and philosophies to meet their own purposes. <br /><br />This was an amazing debut novel, and though the main characters were all high school seniors, it didn’t have the feel of a typical young adult novel. As I alluded to, this is a heady read. There is a lot of philosophical discourse, but it’s fascinating. Hopen’s writing is natural, fluid, and witty. These kids are brilliant young people, but they are privileged, with their lavish parties, drugs, and Ivy League school applications. The tension rises throughout the book as they take more and more risks until the stakes become too great.<br /><br />Thank you to @eccobooks @davidahopen @netgalley for an advance copy for review. <br /><br />The Orchard is available today!<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4152163270360638900" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
