<div id="review_3605198213" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3605198213" />
    <a title="Ailsa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26012725-ailsa"><img alt="Ailsa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605922407p2/26012725.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3605198213?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ailsa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ailsa" href="/user/show/26012725-ailsa">Ailsa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26012725-ailsa?shelf=arcs">arcs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3605198213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11814844102171770047">I was excited when Net Galley approved my request for this book, after I had seen so many of my book-oriented friends speak positively about it. And it did not disappoint!<br/><br/>As a non-Hebrew speaker, I was worried at first how easily the bilingual nature of the text would flow for me. I did find myself stopping to look up words or re-reading sentences to check comprehension. Rather than being annoying, however, I found this experience both rewarding and exciting. What sticks out to me most is how va</span>
  <span id="freeText11814844102171770047" style="display:none">I was excited when Net Galley approved my request for this book, after I had seen so many of my book-oriented friends speak positively about it. And it did not disappoint!<br /><br />As a non-Hebrew speaker, I was worried at first how easily the bilingual nature of the text would flow for me. I did find myself stopping to look up words or re-reading sentences to check comprehension. Rather than being annoying, however, I found this experience both rewarding and exciting. What sticks out to me most is how validating and affirming it can be to see your culture, language, and religion reflected back to you on the pages of the books you read. While the world of The Orchard is one that is unfamiliar to me, I do know what it’s like to not see yourself or your culture or your childhood reflected in dominant narratives of contemporary or historical literature. Growing up in New Zealand, I always get a thrill of exhilaration when I see place names, cultural institutions, or social norms from my life incorporated into the stories I read. It’s a reminder that there are others out there who have lived a life similar to mine and who understand the array of experiences that make up who I am. It’s that moment of seeing yourself in the book you are reading and being reminded that you are not alone. So while my life and my childhood looked very different to Ari’s, I found myself imagining the person who could pick this up and see themselves reflected in his story and in his world. And that’s a very exciting thought. <br /><br />Thanks to Net Galley and Harper Collins Publishing for providing me with an advanced copy of The Orchard in exchange for my honest review </span>
  <a data-text-id="11814844102171770047" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3536513597?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karen Foster" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karen Foster" href="/user/show/6932203-karen-foster">Karen Foster</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3536513597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11528409565770774354">At first I really enjoyed this story of a lonely young man’s journey from his strict Orthodox life in Brooklyn, ruled by ritual, tradition and religious study, to a Jewish academy in the affluent suburbs of Miami. Ari is a sympathetic character and I became truly invested in his story, as he tries to fit in with his spoiled peers and struggles to navigate this alien world of drugs, girls, parties and academic competition. As he becomes part of an elite group of students, cherry-picked to be invo</span>
  <span id="freeText11528409565770774354" style="display:none">At first I really enjoyed this story of a lonely young man’s journey from his strict Orthodox life in Brooklyn, ruled by ritual, tradition and religious study, to a Jewish academy in the affluent suburbs of Miami. Ari is a sympathetic character and I became truly invested in his story, as he tries to fit in with his spoiled peers and struggles to navigate this alien world of drugs, girls, parties and academic competition. As he becomes part of an elite group of students, cherry-picked to be involved in complex philosophical and moral discussions with a respected Rabbi, I found the book lost it’s way. As the group of friends begin to fracture and Evan, their charismatic and troubled leader, begins to push more and more boundaries, I found the story becomes scattered. <br />I had such high hopes for a book compared so favorably to Donna Tartt’s ‘The Secret History’ (which happens to be my favorite book). Sadly I just felt disappointed. There were just too many similarities to that story’s themes and plot points, without ever reaching it’s brilliant heights. 3.5⭐️<br />Thank you to #netgalley and Harper Collins for the Advance copy for review.</span>

<div id="review_3561709778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3561709778" />
    <a title="Kasa Cotugno" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2345554-kasa-cotugno"><img alt="Kasa Cotugno" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483738851p2/2345554.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3561709778?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kasa Cotugno" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kasa Cotugno" href="/user/show/2345554-kasa-cotugno">Kasa Cotugno</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2345554-kasa-cotugno?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2345554-kasa-cotugno?shelf=loc-usa-fl">loc-usa-fl</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2345554-kasa-cotugno?shelf=theme-coming-of-age">theme-coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2345554-kasa-cotugno?shelf=culture-jewish">culture-jewish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3561709778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9198794859365060282">The Orchard acts as a modern interpretation of a parable about 4 sages and their life transformations upon entering an enchanted orchard. Here we have Aryeh Eden, or Ari, raised in the Williamsburg shetl of Brooklyn in an observant Jewish family whose education has been primarily focussed on traditional history and law. His inquisitive mind has even then led to further exploration of more secular subjects, and so when his family is uprooted and plunked down in a "glitzy" Miami suburb, he sees it</span>
  <span id="freeText9198794859365060282" style="display:none">The Orchard acts as a modern interpretation of a parable about 4 sages and their life transformations upon entering an enchanted orchard. Here we have Aryeh Eden, or Ari, raised in the Williamsburg shetl of Brooklyn in an observant Jewish family whose education has been primarily focussed on traditional history and law. His inquisitive mind has even then led to further exploration of more secular subjects, and so when his family is uprooted and plunked down in a "glitzy" Miami suburb, he sees it as a chance for self reinvention. <br /><br />Where this didn't work for me was that after the strict upbringing, the daily observance of ritual and observance, his being given full license to run rampant with the school's fastest group. There was a discrepancy for me in their being on the school's basketball team, which didn't jibe entirely with their hard party personna. What I did like about the book was its description of daily observances and rituals. What didn't work was the sometime repetitive narration that seemed to bloat the middle of the book, which could have used a bit of trimming.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3552630714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3552630714" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68507206-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593022269p2/68507206.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3552630714?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/68507206-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3552630714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17943416394702903083">*4.5*<br/><br/>Thank you to HarperCollins Publishers, Ecco, and NetGalley for providing me with a free copy in return for an honest review.<br/><br/><i>“Divorce yourself from ordinary life and find yourself moving away from being human and toward something… I don’t know, something thrilling and risky and very much divine.”</i><br/><br/>Okay, wow! I just finished this and am still reeling. I had high hopes when I saw this book being compared to The Secret History (a ~dark academia~ novel I enjoyed) and was not disappointed in the sl</span>
  <span id="freeText17943416394702903083" style="display:none">*4.5*<br /><br />Thank you to HarperCollins Publishers, Ecco, and NetGalley for providing me with a free copy in return for an honest review.<br /><br /><i>“Divorce yourself from ordinary life and find yourself moving away from being human and toward something… I don’t know, something thrilling and risky and very much divine.”</i><br /><br />Okay, wow! I just finished this and am still reeling. I had high hopes when I saw this book being compared to The Secret History (a ~dark academia~ novel I enjoyed) and was not disappointed in the slightest.<br /><br />Following Ari Eden, a high school senior uprooted from a sheltered life as an Orthodox Jew in Brooklyn to an upscale Modern Orthodox community in south Florida, this novel is a coming of age that asks the characters and us to consider some of life's greatest moral questions. An outsider in his new home, Ari is quickly absorbed into his prestigious Jewish academy's star group of enigmatic boys that are charming, academically-talented, athletic, and attractive. Through his newfound friendships and special interest from the school’s headmaster, he tests his faith and philosophy in ways he’s never imagined with transformative, yet tragic consequences.<br /><br />Much like The Secret History, the book revolves around a young man that feels like an outsider in a group of magnetic students that meet in a small class to discuss philosophical principles, while engaging in questionable behavior that leads to ~darkness~ ~betrayal~ ~secrets~ and more. I definitely think that if you enjoyed The Secret History, then you will most likely enjoy The Orchard as well, with a few bonuses (positives I found compelling):<br /><br />1. The book is completely based on Jewish culture/lore, which I found incredibly interesting, since orthodox Judaism isn’t a very common backdrop in literature. It helped that I read this on a Kindle, so I could easily access the definitions/translations of all of the Jewish terminology used throughout the narrative. And while it would help to be Jewish to have a better understanding of the culture and the mythos woven into the plot, it is absolutely not necessary to enjoy the story -- it’s a coming of age with universal appeal.<br /><br />2. The characters interested me. I felt for Ari, and his existential dread/malaise spoke to the dissatisfaction I felt in my own teenage years. Each character in the group brought nuance to the story, making the ending have that much more impact (it made me cry.... And I NEVER cry for books, so…).<br /><br />3. The last act was absolutely wild, and I found myself anxiously turning the pages to find out what happens next. It was compelling, beautifully-written, and emotionally impactful. In that respect, I found the ending to be more satisfying than the ending of The Secret History -- it leaves you wondering what actually happened and the meaning of it all.<br /><br />4. The depth of philosophical and literary understanding David Hopen has is astounding. There are so many interesting conversations about different great philosophers and the narrative is embellished frequently with literary quotes (fitting, since the narrator himself is a literary phenom). Whether or not the author was trying to flex his academic muscles, you can tell he did a ton of research and put a lot of work into making his story, and characters, as lush and deep as possible.<br /><br />And while there is A LOT to love and respect about this beautiful novel, it could have been made better by some additional editing of the middle section of the book. It was long and not as interesting as the last part of the story, featuring what felt like many repetitive scenes that could maybe be condensed to offer a tighter reading experience. Other than that, I don’t have anything else to criticize. <br /><br />For fans of literary fiction/campus novels/ ~~dark academia~~ this book should definitely be on your list! I’m eager to see what David Hopen does next.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17943416394702903083" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7042350616416289537">Please visit my book blog <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://cavebookreviews.blogspot.com/'>https://cavebookreviews.blogspot.com/</a>.<br/><br/>As a debut novel, The Orchard is a fascinating coming-of-age story and an elaborate existential treatise. Aryeh (Ari) Eden's life in Brooklyn is one of orthodox Jewish traditions in a community where everyone studies mostly religion with a few secular subjects. Ari gets the shock of his life when his father announces that they are moving to Miami, Florida.<br/>Ari is a thoughtful teenager who loves to read. That passion is fed with more </span>
  <span id="freeText7042350616416289537" style="display:none">Please visit my book blog <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://cavebookreviews.blogspot.com/">https://cavebookreviews.blogspot.com/</a>.<br /><br />As a debut novel, The Orchard is a fascinating coming-of-age story and an elaborate existential treatise. Aryeh (Ari) Eden's life in Brooklyn is one of orthodox Jewish traditions in a community where everyone studies mostly religion with a few secular subjects. Ari gets the shock of his life when his father announces that they are moving to Miami, Florida.<br />Ari is a thoughtful teenager who loves to read. That passion is fed with more substantial classes in English and small philosophy discussions at his new Jewish high school. Girls are another shocking addition to his new school, and Ari's adjustment becomes a very steep learning curve.<br /><br />Ari narrates his journey through his final years in high school in the past tense. He describes his feelings for a lovely young woman, Sophia, and a smart challenger in a cerebral way in the form of Evan, a super brain.<br /><br />Their story and the philosophical exchanges and challenges made this book intense and sometimes difficult to digest.<br /><br />The Orchard is an intellectual debut novel that gave me many moments of questioning the basic existential questions of life. I found the book fascinating, sad, and beautiful. It is in the plus column for readers to enjoy this year.<br /><br />Thank you to the publisher and NetGalley for the opportunity to read an advance</span>

  <span id="freeText4619748929492605537" style="display:none">This was a deep and well thought out story. I loved the coming of age aspect, getting to know the characters and watching their growth.   <span id="freeText17556758744160546501" style="display:none">Raised Catholic with a twelve-year education by priests and nuns, I requested The Orchard in the hopes of broadening my knowledge of Judaism and boy did this deliver! <br /><br />Ari Eden has spent 17 years shrouded in his Jewish culture with zero wiggle room for the modern world around him. <div id="review_3563247766" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3563247766" />
    <a title="jsukolaol.com" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41089204-jsukolaol-com"><img alt="jsukolaol.com" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3563247766?book_show_action=true">Sep 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jsukolaol.com" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jsukolaol.com" href="/user/show/41089204-jsukolaol-com">jsukolaol.com</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3563247766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17808477114713178212">“The Orchard,” by David Hopen is a long-winded, complex, incomprehensible allegorical coming of age story. In this book, Ari Eden, an Orthodox Jewish high school boy moves from his traditional Orthodox Jewish school in Brooklyn to a modern wealthy Orthodox private school in Miami, where he falls in with a popular crowd that drinks and takes drugs. <br/><br/>At the center of the novel is a parable told to Ari and his friends by the school’s head rabbi about four sages that seek God in a paradisiacal orchar</span>
  <span id="freeText17808477114713178212" style="display:none">“The Orchard,” by David Hopen is a long-winded, complex, incomprehensible allegorical coming of age story. In this book, Ari Eden, an Orthodox Jewish high school boy moves from his traditional Orthodox Jewish school in Brooklyn to a modern wealthy Orthodox private school in Miami, where he falls in with a popular crowd that drinks and takes drugs. <br /><br />At the center of the novel is a parable told to Ari and his friends by the school’s head rabbi about four sages that seek God in a paradisiacal orchard of Torah knowledge. Only one comes out unscathed. Ari’s friends apparently represent these four sages in one form or another, but by the time this became apparent my mind was numb from reading this lengthy book, and I was too glad that it was over to ponder the significance of the events that happened at the end of the book.<br /><br />The book details the daily lives of Ari and his new friends, including their excessive partying, drug use and alcohol consumption. In between, there are meetings with the head Rabbi where esoteric, and fairly incomprehensible Torah and Talmud lessons are discussed. <br /><br />The book was rambling and complicated, and hence torturous to read. I wanted to quit at the 16% point, but persevered out of a feeling of obligation to provide a review for the ARC copy I received from NetGalley. On the plus side, the male characters were well developed, if not very likable. (The females all seemed to speak in the same voice—brash and outspoken, bordering on rude and insulting). In sum, the time that it took to read this long-winded book was not worth the effort.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17808477114713178212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3378242954" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3378242954" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/796304-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1360948910p2/796304.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3378242954?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/796304-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3378242954" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7782611761390955356">A well-written book by a young author who seems to really know his topic well. Ari is a high school senior who has left the Orthodox community of New York to join a more liberal/modern Orthodox community in Florida due to his father's job situation. The family moves into the cheapest home in a rich enclave and Ari starts school after being befriended by the very popular boy across the street. For some reason, the smart popular and fast crowd at the school embraces him and we seem him go through </span>
  <span id="freeText7782611761390955356" style="display:none">A well-written book by a young author who seems to really know his topic well. Ari is a high school senior who has left the Orthodox community of New York to join a more liberal/modern Orthodox community in Florida due to his father's job situation. The family moves into the cheapest home in a rich enclave and Ari starts school after being befriended by the very popular boy across the street. For some reason, the smart popular and fast crowd at the school embraces him and we seem him go through the school year being influenced by this clique as he gives up his staid clothing, prayer habits, synagogue attendance, Jewish studies and instead goes with girls, drugs and booze. As a parent you have to wonder at how oblivious his parents are to these changes and why things go so far, but for the sake of the story, it works. The book is filled with intellectual discussion both Jewish and secular and readers may find this fascinating. Hopen appears to know his stuff and it begs the question of how much of this is autobiographical. <br />All in all it is an engrossing book that should get starred reviews and much attention when it is published. I'm grateful to NetGalley for the opportunity to read this book.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3555385803" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3555385803" />
    <a title="Roger" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5246710-roger"><img alt="Roger" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420168170p2/5246710.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3555385803?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger" href="/user/show/5246710-roger">Roger</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3555385803" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10563973933489664318">Thanks to HarperCollins Publishers and NetGalley for providing an advanced copy of this book! <br/><br/>I'm really glad I got to read the first outing of a new author and I'm not disappointed at all. That's not to say the book doesn't have it's flaws, it does, but it's very clear David Hopen has a strong literary voice and had something he wanted to share. <br/><br/>The following is more or less a mish-mash of things I enjoyed and things I didn't. There's no clear outline here so bare with me.<br/><br/>I really enjoyed the s</span>
  <span id="freeText10563973933489664318" style="display:none">Thanks to HarperCollins Publishers and NetGalley for providing an advanced copy of this book! <br /><br />I'm really glad I got to read the first outing of a new author and I'm not disappointed at all. That's not to say the book doesn't have it's flaws, it does, but it's very clear David Hopen has a strong literary voice and had something he wanted to share. <br /><br />The following is more or less a mish-mash of things I enjoyed and things I didn't. There's no clear outline here so bare with me.<br /><br />I really enjoyed the start-up friendliness with our main character's new found "friends". It feels really believable in that he's still a fish out of water around them all but slowly getting a grasp on things. By the time the climax is reached, it really does feel like they had become best friends and were eager to hang out and chill with one another, among lots of drinking and culminating into some surprise drug use.<br /><br />The last 100 pages or so brought upon a pretty melancholic tone which felt pretty gut wrenching at times, even eliciting some of that panicky, heavy breathing I experienced during my read of 'A Little Life'. Not too much of it but it is pretty emotional. And not to say they are comparable, they're absolutely not, but David Hopen knows how to hit those emotional beats and really make them count.<br /><br />It felt a bit inconsistent with what our main character knows about and doesn't. At the beginning he comes across as sheltered and a smarty-pants "good" son, but then he recognizes what Instagram is, acknowledges an area "big enough for beer pong", and thinks about admitting to Sophia that he searched for her on Facebook. He does later admit to "testing the waters" of his faith by exploring different things before the story starts but I still didn't buy it completely.<br /><br />I didn't really enjoy the "opposites" dynamic of Aryeh's father and mother. His father is extremely devout to his faith and wants his son to focus on his religious studies while his mom at every opportunity wants the opposite. ie. the principal of the school gives our main character a book on poetry which the father is "outraged" by because he doesn't know why the principal of a school would provide a text revolved around 'secular studies'. Yet the mom says "...I think it's wonderful he's worldly enough to be teaching things like poetry." The dynamic feels cliche but does start to lighten up a bit as out main character begins to, for lack of better wording, act out not just against his parents but against his faith.<br /><br />There's a thing that happens late in the book that is brought to Aryeh's attention for him to potentially do. He rightfully refuses. Only a couple of pages later his love interest asks him to do it and he just says "sure". I found this frustrating in that he was so adamant about his decision but then his "lover" sticks him with it and he turns into a wet noodle. Aryeh's morals are all over the place which is believable in many points throughout the book, but this is just idiotic. Luckily he does have internal debate over it soon after and reverts back to his starting position, but all of this "drama" could have been saved if he just said no all the way through. Though honestly without this conflict the last part of the book wouldn't have the weight and impact it did so maybe it's accurate depiction is what made me frustrated. <br /><br />Something that A LOT of people have mentioned as a detractor is the consistent "waxing poetic/philosophy" that has to take up somewhere between 50-100 pages of the text. Some of it is pretty engaging but other sections feel like the author is stretching his legs to show off his knowledge on philosophy. It's entirely possible some of it went over my head but a lot of it ultimately didn't help advance the plot so it just felt bloated in that regard. Though I did find it enjoyable to hear about Judaism and just how much it entails. <br /><br />All of this to say that I will absolutely read what David Hopen writes next. His clear, concise voice and strong literary prowess make me excited for it. And I will absolutely make sure to purchase a copy once it's released to the public!<br /><br />4-stars rounded up to 4.5 stars!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10563973933489664318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3571140708" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3571140708" />
    <a title="Terrie Robinson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/104791668-terrie-robinson"><img alt="Terrie Robinson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588271445p2/104791668.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3571140708?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terrie Robinson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terrie Robinson" href="/user/show/104791668-terrie-robinson">Terrie Robinson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/45015116-the-orchard">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">EVERYONE!</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Terrie by: </span>
      NetGalley
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104791668-terrie-robinson?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104791668-terrie-robinson?shelf=advanced-reading-copy">advanced-reading-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104791668-terrie-robinson?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104791668-terrie-robinson?shelf=kindle-e-book">kindle-e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/104791668-terrie-robinson?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3571140708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17649108554729474525">"The Orchard" by David Hopen was a very complex story!<br/><br/>In this coming-of-age story the main character is Ari Eden who is 17 years old and lives in Brooklyn, N.Y. He describes himself as 'existing alone' even when with family and friends. Clearly he's living an uneventful life abiding strict religious rules at a rundown high school within an Orthodox Jewish community. But, Ari doesn't know life can be any different for him, he's never thought about it, he's always lived as he does now. When his fa</span>
  <span id="freeText17649108554729474525" style="display:none">"The Orchard" by David Hopen was a very complex story!<br /><br />In this coming-of-age story the main character is Ari Eden who is 17 years old and lives in Brooklyn, N.Y. He describes himself as 'existing alone' even when with family and friends. Clearly he's living an uneventful life abiding strict religious rules at a rundown high school within an Orthodox Jewish community. But, Ari doesn't know life can be any different for him, he's never thought about it, he's always lived as he does now. When his family moves to Miami, F.L to an affluent Modern Orthodox Jewish community and high school he is shocked by the vast differences in lifestyles between the two. Yes, Ari's life is going to change drastically, in oh-so-many ways!<br /><br />Ari is excited about the opportunity to meet new people, create a new persona, make a new start. But, when faced with this challenge, he fails as he knew he would. He has always felt alone and different. Now Ari understands he will remain this way and acknowledges it as a fact of his life.<br /> <br />Ari's new house is across the street from golden boy Noah Harris, who is also entering his senior year of high school. Noah introduces Ari to his group of friends who begin to welcome him. This clique of rich, intelligent, wild-partying, hard-drinking and drug-taking-kids also takes advantage of Ari's inexperience and quiet, accommodating demeanor. He can't believe all that is happening around him and tries desperately to avoid their bad behavior, holding his own for as long as he can. But peer pressure is a ghastly battle that Ari fights and mostly loses! <br /><br />Ari's new group of friends and his new high school exposes Ari to many different experiences. He begins to blossom in his new learning environment which gives him the confidence to pursue a higher education, something he has never thought about before. He has thoughts of love, something new to Ari who has never even kissed a girl before. Yes, Ari gains greatly but he also loses greatly through this coming-of-age story. His journey is intellectually stimulating, emotionally intense and tragically devastating. <br /> <br />I did find this book to be somewhat cumbersome for me in two areas: <br />1.) There was a language barrier for me. I'm not familiar with Judaism or the vocabulary associated with Yiddish and Hebrew. My Kindle didn't recognized most of the words I attempted to reference and I had no plan or desire to have a dictionary at my side the entire time I was reading this book. <br />2.) The philosophical discussions were way over-the-top for me. I found myself buzzing through most of the Evan &amp; Ari discussions and portions of the Talmudic study group discussions with Rabbi Bloom. <br /><br />With that said, this was a book I wanted to continue reading. I was so unsure how this story would end and I loved that, but when I arrived there, I wanted more! More about Ari, for sure! It's not because I felt the story was lacking depth. No way! I just liked the main character that much and wanted more of him! I'm still thinking about this book and still wanting more over a week later! I hope to see more from this author and I definitely recommend this book!<br />#TheOrchard #NetGalley<br />___________________________<br />Thank you to NetGalley, HarperCollins Publishers and Ecco for an ARC of this book in exchange for an honest review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17649108554729474525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3629020275" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3629020275" />
    <a title="Amey Mahajan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/124094310-amey-mahajan"><img alt="Amey Mahajan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3629020275?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amey Mahajan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amey Mahajan" href="/user/show/124094310-amey-mahajan">Amey Mahajan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3629020275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14094112610218254644">It took me many years to fully recognize the value, entertainment, and enjoyment that fiction books bring to my life. Books like The Orchard, by first-time author David Hopen (27 years young!), are the ones that remind me that I need to be reading fiction.<br/><br/>The Orchard takes us into the world of Kol Neshama — a glitzy, exclusive private yeshiva high school in Florida — through the eyes of Ari Eden, a humble and displaced New Yorker. Ari is a bit of a chameleon. Initially bookish and reserved, and </span>
  <span id="freeText14094112610218254644" style="display:none">It took me many years to fully recognize the value, entertainment, and enjoyment that fiction books bring to my life. Books like The Orchard, by first-time author David Hopen (27 years young!), are the ones that remind me that I need to be reading fiction.<br /><br />The Orchard takes us into the world of Kol Neshama — a glitzy, exclusive private yeshiva high school in Florida — through the eyes of Ari Eden, a humble and displaced New Yorker. Ari is a bit of a chameleon. Initially bookish and reserved, and deeply tied to his strict Orthodox Judaism practice, he is yanked out of his shell by his new Kol Neshama friends and into a new universe of uncertain morality, lavish wealth and religious exploration.<br />Ari’s attempt to balance the pieces of himself — his commitment to Orthodox Judaism, his Lonely Boy aspiration for social status, his genuine desire to explore meaning and truth through literature and sometimes through alcohol — is executed sublimely by Hopen. Ari tries to reconcile his own actions, not fully understanding them even after the fact, while also trying to chart out his future — a perfect storm that the coming-of-age/leaving-the-fold paradox is for so many of us.<br /><br />Hopen is clearly inspired by Donna Tartt’s landmark The Secret History (which I loved), but he establishes himself on his own two feet. Most notably, The Orchard reads like a thriller, with rhythmic prose and a sense of urgency and momentum — but without sacrificing depth (narrative dialogue about Hume does not feel out of place!). Those inclined to thinking about Big Questions through literature will be left happy, as Ari and his classmates’ exploration of religion and truth through great works of the western canon is a highlight of the novel.<br /><br />My gripes are few. I often found myself reaching for Google to make sure I had Hebrew terms correct — but a good learning opportunity for me nonetheless. Ultimately, the character of Sophia, a key figure and love interest in the book, eluded me throughout The Orchard. She seems quintessentially unreachable, yet the protagonists continue to reach. While this is a common theme among young characters in fiction, it’s not one that has ever gelled for me. Some reviews have opined that the novel felt like “a different book” starting around the halfway point. I disagree: the pace and intensity do pick up greatly around the halfway point, but against the backdrop of character evolution, this is not unreasonable.<br /><br />In summary, The Orchard is exactly what I wanted it to be: inspired by The Secret History, but perhaps more philosophical in nature and probing of the Big Questions that dog us all. I’m thankful that this novel doesn’t fall into the tropes of the “leaving-the-fold” genre, nor does it cling to any one aspect of its inspirations. It’s happy to be its own multifarious initiative. Kudos to David Hopen for an impressive debut.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14094112610218254644" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

Information technology has improved rapidly, but there is no reason why progress should be limited to computers or Silicon Valley. Progress can be achieved in any industry or area of business. It comes from the most important skill that every leader must master: learning to think for yourself.\n\nDoing what someone else already knows how to do takes the world from 1 to n, adding more of something familiar. The Lean Startup is a new approach being adopted across the globe, changing the way companies are built and new products are launched. Eric Ries defines a startup as an organization dedicated to creating something new under conditions of extreme uncertainty. This is just as true for one person in a garage or a group of seasoned professionals in a Fortune 500 boardroom. What they have in common is a mission to penetrate that fog of uncertainty to discover a successful path to a sustainable business.\n\n\n\nThe Lean Startup approach fosters companies that are both more capital efficient and that leverage human creativity more effectively. Inspired by lessons from lean manufacturing, it relies on &quot;validated learning,&quot; rapid scientific experimentation, as well as a number of counter-intuitive practices that shorten product development cycles, measure actual progress without resorting to vanity metrics, and learn what customers really want. It enables a company to shift directions with agility, altering plans inch by inch, minute by minute.\n\nRather than wasting time creating elaborate business plans, The Lean Startup offers entrepreneurs - in companies of all sizes - a way to test their vision continuously, to adapt and adjust before it\'s too late. That, of course, is a matter of opinion. And there are other layers to the story.\n\nWith a combination of novelistic flair and historical accuracy, Kate Williams begins by relating the heartbreaking story of Princess Charlotte, the Queen who never was, and her impact on the young Victoria. Our perception of Victoria the Queen is coloured by portraits of her older, widowed self - her dour expression embodying the repressive morality propagated in her time. But Becoming Queen reveals an energetic and vibrant woman, determined to battle for power. It also documents the Byzantine machinations behind Victoria\'s quest to occupy the throne, and shows how her struggles did not end when finally the crown was placed on her head.\n\nIn the late eighteenth century, monarchies were in crisis across Europe. Discontented with their mad King, George III, and his spendthrift offspring, the English pinned their hopes on the only legitimate grandchild: Princess Charlotte, daughter of George, Prince of Wales. But Charlotte died at the age of twenty-two, a few hours after giving birth to a stillborn son. A grieving nation immediately began venerating her as someone who would have made an ideal Queen while Charlotte\'s rackety uncles embarked on a race to produce the next heir.\n\nNo one thought that little Victoria, daughter of the Duke of Kent, would ascend the throne. She, in turn, became increasingly determined to take control of her own destiny, and clashed constantly not only with her hugely ambitious mother but with her protégé and household comptroller, the Irish adventurer, John Conroy. After she became Queen, ministers, even her beloved Prince Albert, still attempted to steal power away from her.\n\nRevealing how Charlotte\'s death shaped Victoria\'s reign and laying bare the passions that swirled around the throne, Becoming Queen is an absorbingly dramatic tale of secrets, sexual repression and endless conflict. After her lauded biography of Emma Hamilton, England\'s Mistress, Kate Williams has produced a most original and intimate portrait of Great Britain\'s longest reigning monarch.<\/span>\n  <a In the first telling the body was nameless. The story was this: a Harvard student had had an affair with her professor, and the professor had murdered her in the Peabody Museum of Archaeology and Ethnology because she\'d threatened to talk about the affair.
